Interpretations of Guidance and Misguidance in Mu'tazilite Thought
Mu'tazilite redefinition of guidance and misguidance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite interpretations of divine guidance and misguidance as reported by al-Jubbai, and the linguistic argument used to support the idea that 'adalla' means naming someone astray rather than creating misguidance.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites reinterpret divine misguidance as God naming or finding a person astray, not creating the act of going astray.

In this continuation, Imam Nasafi presents specific Mu'tazilite interpretations of two key terms: guidance (huda) and misguidance (idalal) as attributed to God. Citing al-Jubbai, he explains that guidance refers to guiding the righteous to Paradise in the Hereafter. For misguidance, the Mu'tazilites argue that it does not involve God creating the act of going astray, but rather God naming the person as 'astray' or finding them astray. To support this linguistic claim, they quote classical Arabic poetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Focus on the two interpretations.
2
Understand the interpretation of guidance
The Mu'tazilites say that guidance attributed to God means guiding the righteous to Paradise in the Hereafter. Note that this is reported from al-Jubbai.
This restricts divine guidance to a specific context.
3
Analyze the interpretation of misguidance
They say 'idalal' is not creating the act of misguidance but rather God naming the person astray (tasmīyah) or finding them astray.
This preserves God's justice by not attributing the creation of sin to Him.
4
Examine the poetic evidence
The author quotes two lines of poetry: one from al-Kumayt using 'akfaronī' (they named me an unbeliever) and one from Tarafa using 'asharranī' (he named me evil). The Mu'tazilites use these to argue that verbs like 'adalla' can mean 'to name someone as such'.
Also note the alternative interpretation: 'found him astray' with the example 'anḥaltu'.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this reinterpretation matters: it avoids attributing the creation of disbelief or sin to God, thereby upholding divine justice.

Source Text

المعاني وإنما وجدت عند التكليف فتضاف إليه، وإن لم يكن منه فيها فعل كما يضاف إلى القرآن: فَزَادَهُمْ إِيمَنَا ) (١) فَزَادَتْهُمْ رِجْسًا (۲) وإلى الدعاء أنه: (وَزَادَهُمْ ) تفورا : ((٣) وإلى الأصنام : إِنَّهُنَّ أَضْلَلْنَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ النَّاسِ ) (٤) وقد يقولون: إنها تضاف إليه لأنها حملت عقيب أحوال أوجدها الله تعالى تصير تلك الأحوال حاملة لهم عليها من نحو الإنعام والإمهال مع علمهم بقدرته، والتقرر عندهم أن من عصى من له القدرة عليه لا يمهله بل يعالجه بالتعذيب والتنكيل، ثم الله تعالى لم يعاقبهم بل أمهلهم، ولم يكتف بالإمهال حتى أدر عليهم سوابغ نعمه وتركهم يتقلبون في آثار أفضاله ويترددون في أثناء منته وإنعامه، فظنوا أن ذلك لرضا الله تعالى بصنيعهم واستحسانه ما هم عليه من النحل والمذاهب حتى ادعوا الأمر فقالوا والله أمرنا بها(ه), فصار ذلك مبعثةً لهم على التمسك بما هم عليه فأضيف إليه كما يضاف إلى الدنيا الغرور لاغترار الناس بها لما هي عليه ) (٦) من حالة الزهرة والبهجة، وربما يقولون ما يضاف إليه من الهداية فالمراد منه هداية المحسنين طريق الجنة في الآخرة، وهذا التأويل محكي عن الجبائي، ويقولون في معنى الإضلال المضاف إلى الله تعالى : إنه ليس بتخليق فعل الضلال بل هو تسميته إياه ضالاً يقال: أضله أي سماه أي سماه ضالاً. (۷) قال الكميت : فطائفة قد أكفروني بحبكم وطائفة قالوا مسيء ومذنب قوله (۱): أكفروني أي سموني كافراً. وقال طرفة(۲): وما (۳) زال شربي الراح حتى أشرني خليلي وحتى ساءني بعض ذلك أي سماني بشرير، أو ربما يقولون: معناه وجده ضالاً يقال : أنحلت فلانا وأجبنته أي وجدته نحيلاً جبانًا.
The meanings [of actions] only occur at the time of obligation (taklif), so they are attributed to Him, even though there is no act from Him in them, just as it is attributed to the Qur'an: 'So it increased them in faith' (1) 'and increased them in abomination' (2), and to supplication that it 'increased them in aversion' (3), and to the idols: 'Indeed, they have led many people astray' (4). And they say: They are attributed to Him because they occur after conditions that God Almighty created, and those conditions become carriers for them, such as bestowal of favors and respite despite their knowledge of His power, and the established notion among them that whoever disobeys one who has power over him will not be given respite but will be immediately punished and tortured. Then God Almighty did not punish them but gave them respite, and did not suffice with respite until He poured upon them abundant blessings and left them to turn over in the traces of His favors and to wander amidst His bounty and beneficence. So they thought that this was due to God Almighty's pleasure with their deed and His approval of the creeds and doctrines they were upon, until they claimed the command and said: 'By God, He commanded us to do it.' So that became a motivation for them to cling to what they were upon, so it was attributed to Him just as the world is attributed to deception (al-ghurur) because people are deceived by it due to what it possesses of bloom and splendor. And perhaps they say: What is attributed to Him of guidance (huda) means the guidance of the righteous to the path of Paradise in the Hereafter. This interpretation is reported from al-Jubbai. And they say concerning the meaning of misguidance (idalal) attributed to God Almighty: It is not the creation of the act of misguidance, but rather His naming him 'astray'. It is said: 'adallahu' meaning 'he named him astray'. (7) Al-Kumayt said: 'A group has declared me an unbeliever for loving you, and a group said: sinful and blameworthy.' His saying (1): 'akfaronī' means 'they named me an unbeliever'. And Tarafa said (2): 'And my drinking of wine did not cease until my companion called me evil and until some of that displeased me' meaning 'he named me evil'. Or perhaps they say: Its meaning is 'he found him astray'. It is said: 'anḥaltu fulānan wa-ajbantuhu' meaning 'I found him emaciated and cowardly'.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage explains how actions and events that are not directly from God are nevertheless attributed to Him in the Qur'an and in common speech. The author begins by noting that certain 'meanings' (al-ma'ani) exist only at the time of obligation (taklif) and are attributed to God even though He does not perform them. He provides Qur'anic examples: increase in faith or abomination is attributed to the Qur'an, aversion to supplication, and misguidance to idols. Then he presents the Mu'tazilite explanation: these things are attributed to God because they follow conditions God creates—such as granting favors and respite despite the people's knowledge of His power. The people assume that if God were displeased, He would punish immediately, but instead He continues to bless them, so they mistakenly think God approves of their ways. Consequently, they claim that God commanded them. Then the author notes that the same logic applies to worldly deception (al-ghurur): the world is called 'deceptive' because of its attractive appearance. He then cites a specific Mu'tazilite interpretation from al-Jubbai: 'guidance' attributed to God means guiding the righteous to Paradise in the afterlife. Likewise, 'misguidance' attributed to God does not mean He creates the act of going astray, but rather that He names the person astray. To support this linguistic point, the author quotes two lines of Arabic poetry: one by al-Kumayt using 'akfaronī' (they called me an unbeliever) and one by Tarafa using 'asharranī' (he called me evil). The Mu'tazilites argue that such expressions show that 'adalla' can mean 'named astray' or 'found astray', not 'created misguidance'.

Key Terms
الهداية
الإضلال
تسمية
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'God leads astray' means God forces people to go astray.
Correction: The passage shows the Mu'tazilites correct this by asserting it only means naming or finding them astray, preserving God's justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite interpretation, what does the attribution of misguidance (idalal) to God actually mean?
A) God creates the act of misguidance in the person.
B) God names the person astray or finds him astray.
C) God leads the person to the path of Paradise.
D) God punishes the person for his misguidance.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilites reinterpret divine misguidance as a linguistic act of naming or finding one astray, not as creating the sin, to preserve God's justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God led me astray' or 'This was written for me'? What does the Mu'tazilite insistence that God only names, not creates, misguidance demand from you in that moment? Are you willing to take full responsibility for your choices, or will you hide behind divine decree?
?
The Mu'tazilites used poetry to redefine God's actions to fit their sense of justice. Have you ever reinterpreted a clear verse or hadith to make it more palatable to your own sense of fairness? Could your understanding of God be shaped more by what feels right to you than by what revelation actually says?
?
If God's 'misguidance' is merely a declaration of your state, then your disbelief or sin is entirely your own doing—there is no one to blame. Does that truth frighten you into repentance, or does it push you to make excuses for your weaknesses? Will you let this lesson strip away your excuses and drive you to seek God's mercy while you still can?

Majority Mu'tazila View on God's Actions
States the majority Mu'tazila position that God does not do what is harmful but does what is beneficial to the fullest extent of His power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Mu'tazila position that God does the utmost benefit possible for every servant, without partiality or harm.

Core Idea
God treats all servants equally in granting the maximum benefit within His power.

This passage presents a central tenet of Mu'tazila theology: divine justice requires God to do what is beneficial for every creature and prevent harm. The Mu'tazila argue that God does not single out prophets for favors unavailable to others; rather, He does the utmost good for everyone, including those who disbelieve. This view has profound implications for understanding divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the words for 'benefit' (مصلحة) and 'harm' (مفسدة).
2
Identify the central claim
The passage makes a clear statement about what God does for every servant. Identify that claim in your own words.
3
Analyze the Prophet vs. Abu Jahl example
The Mu'tazila use this comparison to argue that God does not show partiality. Consider why this example is effective for their argument.
What does this example imply about the nature of divine grace?
4
Understand the conclusion about harm
The passage asserts that God absolutely cannot do harm. Think about how this fits with the overall idea of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه، وفعل بكل عبد مؤمن أو كافر غاية ما هو في مقدوره من مصلحته، وكما فعل بالنبي عليه السلام ما هو في مقدوره من المصلحة فعل بأبي جهل مثله وليس له على النبي محمد عليه السلام إنعام ليس ذلك على أبي جهل، ولو كان ذلك لكان ظالمًا فيما فعل جائرًا مجابيًا بل فعل غاية ما في مقدوره من مصلحة أبي جهل وليس له أن يفعل بأحد ما هو المفسدة له البتة، هذا هو قول جمهورهم.
And He does with every believing or disbelieving servant the utmost of what is in His power of benefit for him. And just as He did with the Prophet (peace be upon him) what is in His power of benefit, He did with Abu Jahl the like of it. And He does not have an favor upon the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that is not upon Abu Jahl. And if that were the case, He would be unjust in what He did, tyrannical, and partial. Rather, He did the utmost of what is in His power of benefit for Abu Jahl, and He absolutely cannot do to anyone what is harmful to him. This is the statement of their majority.
Source pages p. 995

Commentary
This passage presents the majority view of the Mu'tazila regarding God's actions towards His servants. It argues that God treats all individuals—whether believer or disbeliever, prophet or enemy—equally in terms of providing benefit to the fullest extent of His power. The author states that God does not grant Prophet Muhammad any favor that He does not also grant to Abu Jahl; if He did, it would imply injustice or partiality. Instead, God does for Abu Jahl the utmost benefit He can, and it is impossible for God to do what is harmful to anyone. The passage concludes by identifying this as the position of their majority.

Key Terms
مصلحة
مفسدة
جمهور المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila believe God must always grant the most beneficial option (al-aslah).
Correction: This passage shows the majority only require what is beneficial within His power, not necessarily the most beneficial. The phrase 'غاية ما هو في مقدوره من مصلحته' means 'the utmost of what is in His power of benefit', not the absolute best possible.

Checkpoint
According to the majority Mu'tazila view presented in this passage, what does God do for every servant?
A) The utmost of what is in His power of benefit
B) The most beneficial thing possible
C) Whatever the servant deserves
D) Nothing, as God is not obligated

Key Takeaway
The majority Mu'tazila hold that God does the utmost benefit possible for every servant and cannot do harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God has given you the utmost benefit possible in your life, even when you experience pain or loss? If not, what does that reveal about your trust in His justice?
?
How does the Mu'tazila claim that God treats every servant equally—even a prophet and an enemy—challenge your own feelings of entitlement or resentment towards others? Will you let go of comparing your blessings to others?
?
If God never does harm, then what is the source of the hardships you face? Are you willing to see them as part of the benefit God has ordained for you, or do you resist that idea? Commit to one way you will reinterpret a current difficulty as a benefit from God.

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's Doctrine of Lutf
Defines Bishr's view on obligatory versus non-obligatory divine grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's distinction between obligatory benefit (al-salah) and non-obligatory most beneficial (al-aslah), and his doctrine of lutf.

Core Idea
God must grant what benefits, but not the most beneficial, for that would limit His power.

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the head of the Baghdad Mu'tazila, held a nuanced view on divine grace. He argued that while God is obliged to provide what is beneficial for His servants to obey, He is not obliged to provide the most beneficial. This passage lays out his reasoning and distinguishes his school from other Mu'tazili positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the obligation, then the limitation on that obligation.
2
Identify the three key claims
Look for: (1) God must do what is beneficial, (2) God is not obliged to do what is most beneficial, (3) God is capable of a special grace that would lead to faith but is not obliged to give it.
These three points are the core of Bishr's doctrine.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why requiring the most beneficial would imply a limit on God's power. The commentary will help you understand the reasoning.
This connects to the learning objective about preserving God's unlimited power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بشر بن المعتمر رئيس معتزلة بغداد ومن تابعه من أصحابه: إن الله تعالى لا يجوز أن يفعل بعبده ما هو المفسدة له بل يجب عليه أن يفعل به ما هو المصلحة له لكن لا يجب عليه أن يفعل ما هو الأصلح إذ ليس لما في مقدور الله تعالى من المصلحة واللطف غاية لما في القول بإيجاب الأصلح القول بتناهي مقدور الله تعالى: وذلك محال بل هو تعالى قادر على لطف لو فعل لأمنوا اختيارا بهم إيمانًا يستحقون على الله تعالى الثواب مثل ما يستحقونه لو آمنوا مع عدمه، ولا يجب عليه إعطاء ذلك اللطف، وإنما يجب عليه إعطاء ما هو صلاح لهم وإزاحة عللهم فيما يحتاجون إليه لأداء ما كلفهم وما تيسر عليهم مع وجوده العمل بما أمرهم به، وقد فعل ذلك بهم. هذا هو المشهور من مذهبه ومذهب أتباعه، وعامة المعتزلة يسمونهم أصحاب اللطف.
And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the head of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, and those who followed him among his companions, said: Verily, God Almighty is not permitted to do to His servant what is harmful to him; rather, He must do to him what is beneficial for him. But He is not obliged to do what is most beneficial, because there is no limit to what is in God's power of benefit and grace. For the statement that obligates the most beneficial implies the finitude of God's power, and that is impossible. Rather, He Almighty is capable of a grace which, if He did it, they would freely believe with a faith by which they would deserve reward from God similar to what they would deserve if they believed without it. And He is not obliged to grant that grace. Rather, He is obliged to grant what is beneficial for them and to remove their obstacles regarding what they need to perform what He has obligated them, and what makes it easy for them to act upon what He has commanded them. And He has done that for them. This is the well-known (view) of his school and the school of his followers, and the general Mu'tazila call them the 'Companions of Grace' (ashab al-lutf).
Source pages p. 995

Commentary
This passage expounds the doctrine of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the leader of the Baghdad Mu'tazila, and his followers. It defines his position on divine grace (lutf). Bishr asserts that God cannot do what is harmful; He must do what is beneficial. However, He is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah), because that would imply a limit to God's power, which is impossible. Bishr then clarifies that God has the power to grant a specific grace that, if given, would lead people to freely believe and thus deserve reward, but God is not obliged to give that grace. What is obligatory is to grant whatever is beneficial for fulfilling religious duties and to remove obstacles. The passage notes that this school is known as 'ashab al-lutf' (the Companions of Grace) among the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
لطف
مصلحة
أصلح
أصحاب اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bishr held that God must do the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants.
Correction: Bishr explicitly rejects the obligation of al-aslah, arguing it would imply a limit to God's power. He distinguishes between required benefit (salah) and non-required most beneficial (aslah).

Checkpoint
According to Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, why is God not obliged to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah)?
A) Because it would imply a limit to God's power
B) Because God is not wise
C) Because the most beneficial is harmful
D) Because humans do not deserve it

Key Takeaway
Bishr's doctrine of lutf holds that God must provide sufficient benefit for obedience but not the most beneficial, preserving God's unlimited power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God did not give you the 'best' outcome, and complained? After this lesson, do you realize that God only owes you what is sufficient for obedience—not the maximum? How does that confront your sense of entitlement?
?
If God is capable of granting a special grace that would make faith easy but is not obliged to give it, are you using the grace He has already given? Or are you waiting for a perfect sign that may never come? What step of obedience have you been delaying?
?
The Mu'tazila argued God must do what is beneficial for you. If you truly believed that God has already fulfilled His obligation to you, how would your gratitude today change? What specific provision (not 'the best') are you currently taking for granted?

Baghdadi vs Basran Definitions of al-Aslah
Distinguishes the Baghdadi and Basran definitions of al-aslah in the Mu'tazila tradition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing definitions of al-aslah between the Baghdadi and Basran Mu'tazila schools.

Core Idea
Al-aslah means different things to different Mu'tazila schools.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a concise classification of how the Mu'tazila schools defined the key theological concept of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The Baghdadis and Basrans had subtle but important differences. Let's examine the exact wording to grasp these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's wording sink in.
Notice the use of the word 'then' (ثم) indicating a continuation.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives two definitions: one for the Baghdadis and one for some Basrans. Write them down separately.
The Baghdadis tie al-aslah to wisdom and management; the Basrans to benefit.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider how the addition of 'wisdom and management' might change the meaning. Why might the Basrans have omitted it?
This distinction is important for understanding later debates on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصلح عند البغداديين منهم ما هو الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين منهم الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع.
Then, al-aslah according to the Baghdadis among them is what is most beneficial in wisdom and management. And according to some of the Basrans among them, al-salah is benefit and al-aslah is the most beneficial.
Source pages p. 996

Commentary
This passage presents a concise classification of the Mu'tazila positions on the meaning of 'al-aslah' (the most beneficial). According to the Baghdad school, al-aslah is defined in terms of wisdom and management (al-hikma wa al-tadbir), implying a measure of divine discretion. In contrast, some Basrans define al-salah simply as benefit (naf') and al-aslah as the most beneficial (anfa'). This highlights a subtle difference in emphasis between the two schools.

Key Terms

صلاح
حكمة
تدبير
نفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Mu'tazila agreed on the definition of al-aslah.
Correction: This passage shows internal variation: the Baghdadis and Basrans had distinct definitions.

Checkpoint
How do the Baghdadis define al-aslah according to this passage?
A) As the most beneficial in wisdom and management
B) As the most beneficial in terms of reward
C) As the greatest possible benefit
D) As the absence of harm

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila were not monolithic; the Baghdadis and Basrans had distinct definitions of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider what is 'most beneficial' in your life, do you define it as simply what brings you the most immediate benefit, or do you include wisdom and long-term management as the Baghdadis did? How does your definition affect your trust in God's plan?
?
The Basrans equated al-aslah with the most beneficial. Are you willing to accept that God's definition of 'most beneficial' might differ from yours, especially when it involves hardship? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that every event is the most beneficial in God's wise management?
?
The Baghdadis insisted that al-aslah must involve wisdom and management. Do you live as though God's actions are governed by perfect wisdom, even when they seem contrary to your own understanding of benefit? What area of your life most resists this belief?

The Mu'tazila Argument from Wise Command
Presents the Mu'tazila's analogy of a wise commander to argue for obligatory grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's key argument that a wise commander must provide the means for obedience, and how this analogy supports their doctrine of al-aslah.

Core Idea
A wise commander cannot withhold the means to obedience.

This passage presents the central analogy used by the Mu'tazila to argue that God must provide all means for humans to obey His commands. By comparing God to a wise human commander, they build a case that withholding means would contradict wisdom itself. This argument is foundational to their theological system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure of the argument: a conditional statement leading to a conclusion.
2
Identify the conditions
The argument lists several conditions: the commander is wise, he commands obedience, loves and desires it, and is able to provide the means. Notice how each condition builds on the previous one.
The key phrase is 'فلن يجوز أن يمنع' – it is not permissible to prevent.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that it is not permissible for the wise commander to withhold the means to obedience. Ask yourself why this conclusion follows from the conditions.
The argument assumes that providing means does not diminish the commander's wisdom.
4
Reflect on the implied premise
The passage adds that giving the means does not remove the commander from deserving the description of wisdom, and preventing it does not diminish him. This underscores that there is no cost to providing the means.
This premise is crucial to the analogy's force.
5
Consider the theological application
Think about how this analogy would apply to God: if God commands obedience and desires it, He must provide all necessary grace and means. This is the basis of the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهتهم التي يعتمدون عليها أنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا، فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إذا كان قادرا على أن يعطيه ذلك، وكان بذله إياه لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة ومنعه لا ينقصه.
And their argument which they rely on is that we find the wise [commander] when he commands obedience and loves and desires it, it is not permissible for him to prevent the commanded from what leads to his obedience if he is able to give him that, and his giving it does not remove him from deserving the description of wisdom, and preventing it does not diminish him.
Source pages p. 996

Commentary
This passage presents the central argument (shubha) of the Mu'tazila to support their doctrine of al-aslah. The argument is based on an analogy with a wise human commander: if a wise person commands an act, loves it, and desires it, and he has the ability to provide the means for the subordinate to obey without losing his own wisdom, then it would be inconsistent for him to withhold those means. The argument implies that God, being the Wise Commander, must provide the necessary means (including grace) for His commands to be fulfilled. The phrase 'and preventing it does not diminish him' suggests that providing the means does not detract from the commander's wisdom, so there is no reason to withhold.

Key Terms
شبهة
حكيم
طاعة
مأمور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God is not obligated by any external standard of wisdom, but the Mu'tazila argue that wisdom itself demands provision of means.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not claim that God is obligated by an external standard, but that His attribute of wisdom entails that He must act in a certain way, consistent with His nature.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument, what makes it impermissible for a wise commander to withhold the means to obedience?
A) The commander would lose his wisdom if he withheld
B) The commander is able to give the means and giving them does not diminish his wisdom
C) The commanded person deserves the means
D) The commander must always give the most beneficial

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that God, as the Wise Commander, must provide all means necessary for obedience because withholding would contradict wisdom itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to obey God, do you ever blame Him for not providing you with enough means? How does this Mu'tazila argument expose a demand you might be making against God?
?
If you truly believed that God is the Wise Commander who must provide everything you need to obey, would your excuses for sin disappear? What would you need to repent of right now?
?
Are you willing to accept that God may command you to do something without giving you all the means you think you need, and that His wisdom is still perfect? How does your heart wrestle with that trust?

God Always Does What Is Most Beneficial
Nasafi proves that God only does al-aslah using analogies and revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that God always does what is most beneficial for His servants, based on analogies from human behavior and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
God always chooses what is most beneficial for His servants' religion.

This passage presents a powerful analogy: if a person seeking to reconcile an enemy must use the most effective method, then God—who is all-powerful, all-knowing, and unaffected by giving or withholding—must likewise always choose what is most beneficial for His servants. Nasafi supports this with Quranic verses and another analogy from hospitality, concluding that any other action would be unworthy of God's generosity and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical flow from human analogy to divine application.
The passage is dense but coherent.
2
Understand the first analogy
Focus on the analogy of a person with an enemy. Nasafi argues that if two methods are equally easy but one is more effective, choosing the lesser is blameworthy.
This sets the standard for wise action.
3
Apply the analogy to God
See how Nasafi applies this to God: God is powerful, merciful, and generous, and giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him. Therefore, He must always choose the most beneficial action for His servants' religion.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence and final example
Read the quoted verses (7:168 and 7:94) that show God tests people with good and bad to bring them back. Then consider the hospitality analogy that reinforces the point.
The hospitality analogy parallels the first analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا كان له عدو يدعوه إلى موالاته ويجب رجوعه إلى طاعته فلن يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما يعلم أنه أنجع فيما يريد منه، وأدعى له إلى ترك ما هو فيه من عداوته، فإن عرض له أمران من الشدة والغلظة والملاينة والملاطفة يعلم أن أحدهما أدعى لعدوه إلى المراجعة والإنابة، والآخر دون ذلك ففعل الأدون وترك أن يفعل الأصلح الأدعى وكلاهما في قدرته عليهما بمنزلة لا يضره بذلهما ولا ينفعه منعهما، كان عند الحكماء جميعًا مذموماً خارجاً عن استحقاق الوصف بالجود والحكمة، فلما كان هذا فيما بينا على ما وصفنا، وكان الله عز وجل قادرا رحيما جوادًا عالمًا بمواضع حاجة عبادة آمرا لهم بطاعته وترك عداوته والرجوع إلى ولايته لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع ولا يلته منه ذم، علمنا أنه لا يفعل بهم إلا أصلح الأشياء لهم في دينهم وأدعى لهم إلى طاعته سقما كان ذلك أو صحة لذة أو ألمًا، آمنوا أو كفروا، أطاعوا أو عصوا قال الله تعالى: وَبَلَوْنَاهُم بِالْحَسَنَاتِ وَالسَّيِّئَاتِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَرْجِعُونَ وقال تعالى: وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ نَبِيٍّ إِلَّا أَخَذْنَا أَهْلَهَا بِالْبَأْسَاءِ وَالضَّرَّاءِ. دل ذلك أنه يعاملهم بمختلف الأحوال على ما يرى الأصلح لهم والأدعى إلى الحق، وربما يوضحون هذا بمن أنجز ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملايته لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر، يجب عليه أن يدعوه ببشر وملاطفة ولو عامله بضد ذلك لما كان حكيمًا، وصار منع ذلك بمنزلة منع التمكين من الحضور، ومنع التمكين عن فعل ما أمره به وذا ليس بحكمة بل هو سفه فكذا هذا، وربما يقولون لو أعطى العبد ما في مقدوره من اللطف انتفع به ولم يتضرر الله تعالى ولو لم يعطه لتضرر العبد وما انتفع الله تعالى بالمنع، ومن منع غيره ما لو أعطى لانتفع به غيره ولو لم يعط لتضرر به غيره، ولا ضرر على المانع بالإعطاء، ولا منفعة له بالمنع لعُدَّ نهاية في البخل والسفه والقساوة والله منزه عن الوصف بهذه الصفات.
And likewise, if a person has an enemy who calls him to his allegiance, and it is obligatory for him to return to his obedience, it is not permissible for him to treat him with harshness or gentleness except with what he knows is most effective in what he wants from him and more conducive for him to abandon his enmity. If two matters present themselves, one of severity and harshness and the other of gentleness and kindness, and he knows that one is more conducive for his enemy to return and repent, while the other is less so, and he does the lesser and leaves the more beneficial and more conducive, while both are within his power in a way that neither giving nor withholding harms or benefits him, he would be considered blameworthy by all wise people, outside the merit of being described as generous and wise. Since this is as we have described, and God Almighty is powerful, merciful, generous, knowing the places of need of His servants, commanding them to obey Him and abandon enmity and return to His allegiance, and giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, nor does blame befall Him, we know that He only does for them the most beneficial things for their religion and most conducive to His obedience, whether that be sickness or health, pleasure or pain, whether they believe or disbelieve, obey or disobey. Allah said: 'And We tried them with good things and evil that perhaps they would return.' And He said: 'And We sent not a prophet to any city but We seized its people with destitution and hardship.' That indicates that He treats them with varying conditions according to what He sees as most beneficial for them and most conducive to the truth. And perhaps they clarify this with the example of a man who prepared hospitality for another man and commanded him to attend and wanted that, and he knew that if he invited him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he did that with harshness and frown he would not attend. He must invite him with cheerfulness and kindness, and if he treated him opposite to that he would not be wise. And preventing that is like preventing enabling him to attend, and preventing enabling him to do what he commanded him, and that is not wisdom but foolishness. So likewise this. And perhaps they say: If the servant gave what was in his power of kindness, the other would benefit and Allah would not be harmed, and if he did not give, the servant would be harmed and Allah would not benefit from withholding. And whoever withholds from another what if he gave the other would benefit, and if he did not give the other would be harmed, and there is no harm to the giver in giving nor benefit in withholding, he would be considered extreme in miserliness, foolishness, and harshness. And Allah is exalted above being described with these attributes.
Source pages p. 997

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God only does what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. The author begins with an analogy: if a person has an enemy whom he wants to reconcile, he must choose the most effective method (harsh or gentle) to achieve his goal. If he has two options—one more effective and one less—and he chooses the lesser, even though both are equally easy for him, he is considered blameworthy and lacking in generosity and wisdom. The author then applies this to God: God is powerful, merciful, generous, and knows His servants' needs. He commands them to obey Him and abandon enmity. Since giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, He must always choose the most beneficial action for them in their religion, whether that involves hardship or ease, belief or disbelief. He cites Quranic verses (7:168 and 7:94) to show that God tests people with good and bad to bring them back. The author then offers another analogy: a host who invites a guest knows that a cheerful invitation will make the guest come, while a harsh one will deter him. The wise host must invite cheerfully. Similarly, God must provide what is conducive to obedience. The passage concludes that withholding kindness when it would benefit the other without harming oneself is extreme miserliness and foolishness, which God is free from.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aslah)
الجود (al-jud)
الحكمة (al-hikmah)
القساوة (al-qasawah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God sometimes does what is not beneficial for His servants, such as allowing suffering.
Correction: The passage clarifies that even hardships are most beneficial for one's religion because they may lead to repentance and return to obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must God always choose the most beneficial action for His servants?
A) Because He fears harm from His servants
B) Because giving and withholding benefit or harm Him
C) Because He is powerful, merciful, and generous, and neither giving nor withholding affects Him
D) Because He is obligated by human reason

Key Takeaway
God always does what is most beneficial for His servants' religion, even if it appears otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When hardship strikes, do you truly believe God is choosing the most beneficial thing for your religion, or do you secretly doubt His wisdom? What would it take for your heart to fully accept His decree as al-aslah?
?
The passage says God uses both good and evil to bring people back. Which recent trial or blessing did you dismiss as random or unfair? How was that moment actually an invitation to return to Him?
?
If you were the host inviting a guest, you would choose kindness. In your own efforts to invite others to good, do you choose the most effective method, or convenience? How does your behavior reflect (or fail to reflect) the attribute of hikmah?

The Four Proofs and Quranic Argument
Nasafi lists the four proofs and elaborates on the Quranic argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four pillars of the Ahl al-Haqq proof and how the Quranic verses refute the Mu'tazili view on divine will and human guidance.

Core Idea
God's will in the Quran proves He could guide all, but He does not.

Imam Nasafi begins by laying out the comprehensive proof of the Ahl al-Haqq, which rests on four foundations: the Quran, observable existence, the consensus of religious communities, and rational evidence. He then focuses on the Quran, citing three clear verses that affirm God's absolute will and power over guidance, and he refutes the Mu'tazili reinterpretation that reduces these verses to mere compulsion. This lesson establishes the scriptural basis for the Sunni position that God's actions are not bound by what is most beneficial for His creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that Nasafi lists four sources of proof. Underline them in the text.
The four are: Kitāb Allāh, al-Wujūd, Ijmāʿ ahl al-adyān, al-Dalīl al-ʿaqlī.
2
Examine the three Quranic verses
Nasafi cites three verses. Read each one and note the common theme: God's will to guide all if He so desired.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazili interpretation
Nasafi argues that if God could not actually make people believe, the verses would be false boasts. He rejects the idea that the verses refer to compulsion (jabr/qasr).
This is a key logical argument: the verses affirm power, not compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل لأهل الحق في المسألة كتاب الله تعالى والوجود وإجماع أهل الأديان والدليل العقلي. أما الكتاب فقول الله تعالى: وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا وقوله تعالى: فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ وقوله تعالى: وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَنْ فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا. ولو لم يكن في مقدوره ما لو فعل بهم لآمنوا لم يكن لهذه الآيات فائدة سوى ادعاء قدرة ومشيئة ليستا له كفعل الكذوب المتصلف الذي يتحلى بما ليس فيه. وحملهم الآيات على مشيئة الجبر والقسر باطل على ما مر. وأما الوجود فإن الكفر والمعاصي قد وجدت، وإنا أثبتنا بالدلائل السمعية والعقلية التي لا مدفع لها ولا اعتراض عليها ولا ريب فيها لمن أنصف من نفسه ولم يكابر عقله أن أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله، وفيها الكفر والمعاصي، وهم يتضررون بها ولا ينتفعون فلم يكن إيجادها مصلحة لهم فضلاً عن الأصلح. وهذه المسألة في الحقيقة فرع لتلك المسألة. ثم ما هو أظهر من هذا وأدل أن الله تعالى فعل بالكافر ما لا صلاح له فيه بل له فيه مضرة ومفسدة، فإن الله تعالى أبقاه إلى وقت بلوغه وركب فيه العقل مع علمه أنه لا يؤمن بل يكفر ويعادي الله تعالى، ولا شك أن الله تعالى إذا علم أنه يكفر عند بلوغه واعتدال عقله لو أماته في حال صغره وعدم تمييزه أو لم يركب فيه العقل عند بلوغه حتى لو بلغ مجنوناً غير مخاطب لكان ذلك أصلح له، وحيث لم يمته بل أبقاه وركب فيه العقل والتمييز حتى دخل في حد التكليف والامتحان مع علمه أنه يكفر دل أنه لم يفعل به ما له فيه صلاح. وكذا من عاش مدة على الإسلام ثم ارتد بعد ذلك ولو كان الله تعالى قبض روحه وتوفاه قبل ارتداده بساعة حتى ختم له بالإسلام ولم يستحق التعذيب في النار خالداً مخلداً كان أصلح له، وحيث لم يفعل بل أبقاه مع علمه أنه يرتد عن الإسلام وكان ذلك مضرة له لا صلاحاً فقد فعل ذلك وهو تعالى حكيم، دل أن ذلك كان حكمة، ووقعت المعتزلة فيما وقعت لجهلهم بحقيقة الحكمة. ثم بعد تقرير فعل الله تعالى ذلك دعوى من زعم أن ذلك سفه وليس بحكمة وصف منه الله تعالى بذلك وهو كفر، بل ظهر بفعله أنه حكمة وإن جهلت المعتزلة بجهة الحكمة إذ الجهل عليهم جائز وخروج فعل الله تعالى عن الحكمة ممتنع. يحققه أن الله تعالى أضاف بقاءهم على الكفر إلى إبقائه وإمهاله بقوله تعالى إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا.
And the proof for the people of truth in the matter is the Book of God Almighty, [the evidence from] existence, the consensus of the people of religions, and rational proof. As for the Book, it is the saying of God Almighty: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' and His saying: 'And if He had willed, He would have guided you all' and His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them together.' And if it were not within His power that which if He did to them they would believe, these verses would have no benefit except claiming a power and will that are not His, like the act of a lying pretender who adorns himself with what he does not possess. And their interpretation of the verses as [referring to] a will of compulsion and coercion is false, as has preceded. As for [the evidence from] existence: disbelief and sins have indeed occurred, and we have proven by textual and rational proofs—which cannot be refuted nor objected to, and in which there is no doubt for one who is fair to himself and does not argue with his intellect—that the actions of servants are created by God, and among them are disbelief and sins, and they are harmed by them and not benefited, so their creation was not a benefit for them, let alone the most beneficial. And this issue is in reality a branch of that issue. Then what is more apparent than this and more indicative is that God Almighty did to the disbeliever what has no benefit for him, rather there is in it harm and corruption. For God Almighty kept him alive until the time of his maturity and placed in him reason, while knowing that he will not believe but will disbelieve and become an enemy of God Almighty. There is no doubt that if God Almighty knew that he would disbelieve at his maturity and the soundness of his reason, had He caused him to die in his childhood and lack of discernment, or had He not placed in him reason upon his maturity so that he would become insane and not obligated, that would have been more beneficial for him. And since He did not cause him to die, but kept him alive and placed in him reason and discernment until he entered the realm of obligation and trial, while knowing that he would disbelieve, it indicates that He did not do for him what has benefit for him. Similarly, one who lived for a period upon Islam and then apostatized afterwards—we seek refuge in God—if God Almighty had taken his soul and caused him to die an hour before his apostasy, so that he would be sealed with Islam and not deserve eternal torment in the Fire, that would have been more beneficial for him. And since He did not do so, but kept him alive while knowing that he would apostatize from Islam and that that would be harm for him and not benefit, He did that while He is Wise. It indicates that that was wisdom, and the Mu'tazila fell into what they fell into due to their ignorance of the reality of wisdom. Then, after establishing that God Almighty did that, the claim of one who claims that that is foolishness and not wisdom, describing God Almighty with that, is disbelief. Rather, it appeared by His action that it is wisdom, even if the Mu'tazila are ignorant of the aspect of wisdom, for ignorance is possible for them, and the departure of God's action from wisdom is impossible. And it is confirmed that God Almighty attributed their persistence in disbelief to His keeping them and granting them respite by His saying: 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin.'
Source pages p. 998

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) against the Mu'tazila on the issue of whether God must do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by stating that the proof rests on four pillars: the Book of God, empirical existence, the consensus of religious communities, and rational proof. He first cites three Quranic verses (32:13, 6:149, 10:99) that state if God willed, He would have guided all people. He argues that if it were not in God's power to make them believe, these verses would be meaningless—like a boast of power one does not possess. He rejects the Mu'tazili reinterpretation of these verses as referring to compulsion, noting it is already refuted. Next, he turns to empirical evidence: disbelief and sins exist, and it is established through decisive textual and rational proofs that human actions are created by God. Since these actions harm the doer and do not benefit him, their creation cannot be a benefit (maṣlaḥa) for him, let alone the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). He then sharpens the argument with two concrete examples. First, God keeps a disbeliever alive until maturity and gives him reason, knowing he will disbelieve. It would have been more beneficial for that person to die as a child or to be insane and thus not obligated. The fact that God does not do so shows He does not always do what is most beneficial for the creature. Second, similarly, God preserves a Muslim who later apostatizes, even though it would have been better for him to die before apostasy. Since God is wise, this proves that such actions are wise even if we do not grasp the wisdom. He then warns that calling such divine action foolish is itself disbelief. The Mu'tazila's error stems from ignorance of the true nature of wisdom. Finally, he corroborates with the verse Āl ʿImrān 3:178, where God attributes the disbelievers' persistence in sin to His granting them respite, affirming that this is a purposeful action.

Key Terms
دليل أهل الحق
كتاب الله
الوجود
إجماع أهل الأديان
الدليل العقلي
جبر
قسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quranic verses about God's will to guide all people imply that God compels people to believe or disbelieve, removing human responsibility.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the verses affirm God's power and will, but He does not compel; rather, He leaves people to choose, and the verses highlight His sovereignty. The Mu'tazili interpretation of compulsion is a distortion.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument Nasafi makes using the three Quranic verses?
A) God compels people to disbelieve.
B) If God lacked the power to make people believe, the verses would be meaningless boasts.
C) The verses prove that humans create their own actions.
D) The verses are about the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The Quran affirms God's absolute power to guide all, undermining the Mu'tazili claim that God must do what is most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God could guide everyone but does not, do you ever wonder why He guided you? What does that undeserved guidance demand from you today?
?
In what areas of your life do you secretly assume God owes you something—that He must act in a certain way? How does this passage challenge that assumption and call you to submit to His absolute will?
?
If these Quranic verses were meaningless without God's actual power to guide, how seriously do you take His words in your daily life? Do you treat the Quran as a reflection of His absolute will or as just a book of advice?

The Existence Argument: Disbelief and Its Harm
Nasafi presents the proof from existence and the first illustrative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of disbelief and sins, combined with the fact that they harm the doer, disproves the Mu'tazili doctrine that God must create only what is beneficial or most beneficial.

Core Idea
Disbelief exists and harms its doer, proving God does not always create benefit.

Having established the Quranic proof, Nasafi now turns to the second evidence: the observable reality of disbelief and sins. He asserts that it is proven by indisputable textual and rational proofs that human actions are created by God, and among these actions are disbelief and sins that harm the doer. Since these actions provide no benefit to the doer, their creation cannot be described as a benefit (maṣlaḥa), let alone the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). He then deepens the argument with a concrete example: God keeps a disbeliever alive and gives him reason, knowing he will disbelieve, whereas it would have been more beneficial for that person to die as a child or be insane. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the existence proof section
Read the passage from 'وأما الوجود' to the end of the first example. Note how Nasafi establishes that disbelief and sins exist and are created by God.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Nasafi says the actions are created by God, and they harm the doer. Therefore, they are not a benefit for him.
This directly challenges the Mu'tazili claim of al-aṣlaḥ.
3
Work through the illustrative example
Read the example of the disbeliever kept alive and given reason. Why does Nasafi say it would have been better for him to die as a child or be insane?
Because then he would not be obligated and would avoid eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل لأهل الحق في المسألة كتاب الله تعالى والوجود وإجماع أهل الأديان والدليل العقلي. أما الكتاب فقول الله تعالى: وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا وقوله تعالى: فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ وقوله تعالى: وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَنْ فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا. ولو لم يكن في مقدوره ما لو فعل بهم لآمنوا لم يكن لهذه الآيات فائدة سوى ادعاء قدرة ومشيئة ليستا له كفعل الكذوب المتصلف الذي يتحلى بما ليس فيه. وحملهم الآيات على مشيئة الجبر والقسر باطل على ما مر. وأما الوجود فإن الكفر والمعاصي قد وجدت، وإنا أثبتنا بالدلائل السمعية والعقلية التي لا مدفع لها ولا اعتراض عليها ولا ريب فيها لمن أنصف من نفسه ولم يكابر عقله أن أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله، وفيها الكفر والمعاصي، وهم يتضررون بها ولا ينتفعون فلم يكن إيجادها مصلحة لهم فضلاً عن الأصلح. وهذه المسألة في الحقيقة فرع لتلك المسألة. ثم ما هو أظهر من هذا وأدل أن الله تعالى فعل بالكافر ما لا صلاح له فيه بل له فيه مضرة ومفسدة، فإن الله تعالى أبقاه إلى وقت بلوغه وركب فيه العقل مع علمه أنه لا يؤمن بل يكفر ويعادي الله تعالى، ولا شك أن الله تعالى إذا علم أنه يكفر عند بلوغه واعتدال عقله لو أماته في حال صغره وعدم تمييزه أو لم يركب فيه العقل عند بلوغه حتى لو بلغ مجنوناً غير مخاطب لكان ذلك أصلح له، وحيث لم يمته بل أبقاه وركب فيه العقل والتمييز حتى دخل في حد التكليف والامتحان مع علمه أنه يكفر دل أنه لم يفعل به ما له فيه صلاح. وكذا من عاش مدة على الإسلام ثم ارتد بعد ذلك ولو كان الله تعالى قبض روحه وتوفاه قبل ارتداده بساعة حتى ختم له بالإسلام ولم يستحق التعذيب في النار خالداً مخلداً كان أصلح له، وحيث لم يفعل بل أبقاه مع علمه أنه يرتد عن الإسلام وكان ذلك مضرة له لا صلاحاً فقد فعل ذلك وهو تعالى حكيم، دل أن ذلك كان حكمة، ووقعت المعتزلة فيما وقعت لجهلهم بحقيقة الحكمة. ثم بعد تقرير فعل الله تعالى ذلك دعوى من زعم أن ذلك سفه وليس بحكمة وصف منه الله تعالى بذلك وهو كفر، بل ظهر بفعله أنه حكمة وإن جهلت المعتزلة بجهة الحكمة إذ الجهل عليهم جائز وخروج فعل الله تعالى عن الحكمة ممتنع. يحققه أن الله تعالى أضاف بقاءهم على الكفر إلى إبقائه وإمهاله بقوله تعالى إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا.
And the proof for the people of truth in the matter is the Book of God Almighty, [the evidence from] existence, the consensus of the people of religions, and rational proof. As for the Book, it is the saying of God Almighty: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' and His saying: 'And if He had willed, He would have guided you all' and His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them together.' And if it were not within His power that which if He did to them they would believe, these verses would have no benefit except claiming a power and will that are not His, like the act of a lying pretender who adorns himself with what he does not possess. And their interpretation of the verses as [referring to] a will of compulsion and coercion is false, as has preceded. As for [the evidence from] existence: disbelief and sins have indeed occurred, and we have proven by textual and rational proofs—which cannot be refuted nor objected to, and in which there is no doubt for one who is fair to himself and does not argue with his intellect—that the actions of servants are created by God, and among them are disbelief and sins, and they are harmed by them and not benefited, so their creation was not a benefit for them, let alone the most beneficial. And this issue is in reality a branch of that issue. Then what is more apparent than this and more indicative is that God Almighty did to the disbeliever what has no benefit for him, rather there is in it harm and corruption. For God Almighty kept him alive until the time of his maturity and placed in him reason, while knowing that he will not believe but will disbelieve and become an enemy of God Almighty. There is no doubt that if God Almighty knew that he would disbelieve at his maturity and the soundness of his reason, had He caused him to die in his childhood and lack of discernment, or had He not placed in him reason upon his maturity so that he would become insane and not obligated, that would have been more beneficial for him. And since He did not cause him to die, but kept him alive and placed in him reason and discernment until he entered the realm of obligation and trial, while knowing that he would disbelieve, it indicates that He did not do for him what has benefit for him. Similarly, one who lived for a period upon Islam and then apostatized afterwards—we seek refuge in God—if God Almighty had taken his soul and caused him to die an hour before his apostasy, so that he would be sealed with Islam and not deserve eternal torment in the Fire, that would have been more beneficial for him. And since He did not do so, but kept him alive while knowing that he would apostatize from Islam and that that would be harm for him and not benefit, He did that while He is Wise. It indicates that that was wisdom, and the Mu'tazila fell into what they fell into due to their ignorance of the reality of wisdom. Then, after establishing that God Almighty did that, the claim of one who claims that that is foolishness and not wisdom, describing God Almighty with that, is disbelief. Rather, it appeared by His action that it is wisdom, even if the Mu'tazila are ignorant of the aspect of wisdom, for ignorance is possible for them, and the departure of God's action from wisdom is impossible. And it is confirmed that God Almighty attributed their persistence in disbelief to His keeping them and granting them respite by His saying: 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin.'
Source pages p. 998

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) against the Mu'tazila on the issue of whether God must do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by stating that the proof rests on four pillars: the Book of God, empirical existence, the consensus of religious communities, and rational proof. He first cites three Quranic verses (32:13, 6:149, 10:99) that state if God willed, He would have guided all people. He argues that if it were not in God's power to make them believe, these verses would be meaningless—like a boast of power one does not possess. He rejects the Mu'tazili reinterpretation of these verses as referring to compulsion, noting it is already refuted. Next, he turns to empirical evidence: disbelief and sins exist, and it is established through decisive textual and rational proofs that human actions are created by God. Since these actions harm the doer and do not benefit him, their creation cannot be a benefit (maṣlaḥa) for him, let alone the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). He then sharpens the argument with two concrete examples. First, God keeps a disbeliever alive until maturity and gives him reason, knowing he will disbelieve. It would have been more beneficial for that person to die as a child or to be insane and thus not obligated. The fact that God does not do so shows He does not always do what is most beneficial for the creature. Second, similarly, God preserves a Muslim who later apostatizes, even though it would have been better for him to die before apostasy. Since God is wise, this proves that such actions are wise even if we do not grasp the wisdom. He then warns that calling such divine action foolish is itself disbelief. The Mu'tazila's error stems from ignorance of the true nature of wisdom. Finally, he corroborates with the verse Āl ʿImrān 3:178, where God attributes the disbelievers' persistence in sin to His granting them respite, affirming that this is a purposeful action.

Key Terms
الوجود
مصلحة
أصلح
حكمة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of evil and suffering proves God is not wise or just.
Correction: Nasafi argues that God's actions are wise and just even if they do not always bring apparent benefit to individuals; the wisdom may be hidden.

Checkpoint
According to the existence proof, why does Nasafi say that keeping a disbeliever alive and giving him reason is evidence against the Mu'tazili view?
A) Because it shows God is not in control.
B) Because it would have been more beneficial for that person to die as a child or be insane, yet God did not do that.
C) Because the disbeliever will eventually believe.
D) Because the disbeliever is not harmed.

Key Takeaway
The existence of disbelief and its harm to the doer proves that God does not always create what is beneficial for the individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God knows that a specific disbeliever will never believe, yet He gives him reason and keeps him alive. If God knows that you might persist in a particular sin, does that make you any less responsible? What does this passage demand you to examine in your own life today?
?
The passage states that the disbeliever is harmed and not benefited by his actions. Think of a habit or sin you continue. Are you truly being benefited by it, or are you harming yourself? What step will you take to break it starting now?
?
Consider the child who dies before accountability—he is saved from eternal punishment. If God had taken you early, you would have avoided many trials. Does the fact that He gave you a longer life with reason make you more grateful or more defiant? How will you respond to His trust in giving you this opportunity?

The Apostate Example and Wisdom Conclusion
Nasafi presents the second example and concludes with the wisdom argument and a Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the apostate further refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine, and how Nasafi concludes that divine actions are wise even if their wisdom is hidden, and that calling them foolish is disbelief.

Core Idea
God's wisdom in allowing apostasy proves His actions are wise, not foolish.

Nasafi continues his refutation with a second example: a Muslim who later apostatizes. He argues that it would have been more beneficial for him to die before apostasy, yet God keeps him alive, knowing the outcome. This again shows that God does not always do what is most beneficial for the individual. Nasafi then draws a sharp conclusion: to claim that such divine actions are foolish (safah) is itself disbelief. The Mu'tazila's error stems from their ignorance of the true nature of divine wisdom. Finally, he reinforces the argument with a Quranic verse that shows God grants respite so that sinners increase in sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the apostate example
Read the passage from 'وكذا من عاش مدة' to the end. Notice the parallel structure with the previous example.
The structure is similar: hypothetical better outcome vs. actual divine action.
2
Understand the wisdom argument
Nasafi says 'دل أن ذلك كان حكمة' (it indicates that was wisdom). He emphasizes that ignorance of wisdom does not negate wisdom.
3
Note the strong warning against calling God foolish
Nasafi states that calling God's action 'safah' (foolishness) is kufr. This is a serious theological boundary.
4
Read the concluding verse
The verse Āl ʿImrān 3:178 is cited to show that God's respite is purposeful, not meaningless.
The verse: 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin.'

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the ruling on someone who claims that God's action of keeping the apostate alive is foolishness (safah)?
A) It is a valid opinion.
B) It is a sin but not disbelief.
C) It is disbelief (kufr).
D) It is a sign of deep thought.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see someone else suffer, do you truly believe that God's wisdom is at work even if you cannot see the benefit? What specific thought or action in your life reveals that you doubt this truth?
?
If God allowed you to persist in a sin for years before you repented, do you recognize that His wisdom is behind that delay? How does that change the way you view your past mistakes?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected this wisdom because it seemed foolish to them. Have you ever rejected a command of God or a divine decree in your heart because you thought it made no sense? Will you commit today to trusting God's wisdom over your own understanding?

The Refutation of the Al-Aslah Principle
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah by showing that human reason cannot determine what is truly beneficial in divine actions, and that the Mu'tazili position leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Human intellect cannot impose what is beneficial on God.

In this passage, al-Nasafi directly confronts the Mu'tazili claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. He argues that this claim is not only false but leads to absurd consequences when applied consistently. By following the exchange, you will see how the Maturidi school defends God's absolute wisdom and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Begin by reading the Mu'tazili argument as presented by al-Nasafi: they claim that God only does what is most beneficial, even if we do not understand it.
Notice how they try to salvage their position after previous refutations.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Nasafi turns their logic against them by proposing that creating disbelief could be most beneficial. Examine how he parallels their own reasoning about divine foreknowledge.
Pay attention to the symmetry he creates between the two cases.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that human intellect cannot fully grasp benefit and harm in divine actions. Consider why this means we should not impose obligations or restrictions on God.
This underscores the Maturidi emphasis on God's wisdom and freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما تحيروا في هذا زعم بعضهم: ثبت لنا أن الله تعالى لا يفعل إلا ما هو الأصلح فيكون جميع ما يفعل أصلح، وإن كنا لا نعقل نحن وجه المصلحة. فيقال لهم: بل يعقل فيه كل عاقل وجه المفسدة والمضرة، وعرف أن ما قلتم باطل. ثم نقول لهم ما أنكرتم على من يقول لكم إذا ثبت بما بينا أنه تعالى حكيم علم أن منع المصلحة والأصلح حكمة وإن كنا لا نعقل وجه الحكمة، ويقال لهم ما أنكرتم على هذا أن يكون تخليق الكفر أصلح للعبد؟ فإن قالوا: كيف يكون ذلك أصلح وقد يتضرر به العبد؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون الإبقاء إلى أن يكفر أو يرتد أصلح له وهو يتضرر به، وإن قالوا: لا يتصور من العبد الإيمان إذا خلق فيه الكفر . قلنا ولا يتصور منه الإيمان إذا علم أنه يكفر فإن كان هذا مصلحة فذاك أيضا مصلحة، وإن لم يكن ذاك مصلحة فهذا أيضا ليس بمصلحة وإن عقلا هذا مبلغ معرفته بالمصلحة والمفسدة لحقيق أن لا يعرف صاحبه به الحكمة والسفه ولحري أن لا يتحكم على الله بالإيجاب عليه تارة والحجر عليه أخرى، والله الموفق.
When they became perplexed about this, some of them claimed: It is established for us that God Most High does not do anything except what is most beneficial, so all that He does is most beneficial, even if we ourselves do not understand the aspect of benefit. Then it is said to them: Rather, every intelligent person understands in it the aspect of harm and injury, and knows that what you say is false. Then we say to them: What do you deny from one who says to you: Since it is established by what we have shown that He Most High is wise—knowledge that withholding benefit and the most beneficial is wisdom, even if we do not understand the aspect of wisdom? And it is said to them: What do you deny from this, that the creation of disbelief could be most beneficial for the servant? For if they say: How can that be most beneficial when the servant is harmed by it? We say: And how can letting him remain until he disbelieves or apostatizes be most beneficial for him when he is harmed by it? And if they say: It is not possible for the servant to have faith if disbelief is created in him. We say: Nor is it possible for him to have faith if He knows that he will disbelieve. For if this is a benefit, then that is also a benefit; and if that is not a benefit, then this is also not a benefit. And if the intellect has reached this limit of its knowledge of benefit and harm, it is worthy that its possessor does not recognize wisdom and foolishness, and it is more worthy that he does not arrogantly presume to impose obligations on God at one time and to restrict Him at another. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation against the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). Some Mu'tazila, perplexed by the previous argument, claim that God does only what is most beneficial, even if we do not understand the benefit. Al-Nasafi responds that every intelligent person can see the harm in the examples given, so their claim is false. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum: If God's withholding benefit can be wisdom, then why cannot creating disbelief (takhliq al-kufr) in a servant be for his own best interest? When the Mu'tazila object that the servant is harmed by disbelief, al-Nasafi retorts that allowing the servant to live until he disbelieves is also harmful. And if they argue that faith is impossible if disbelief is created in him, al-Nasafi points out that by their own doctrine—since God knows he will disbelieve—faith is also impossible. Thus the Mu'tazili position is symmetrical and collapses. He concludes that human intellect cannot fully grasp benefit and harm in divine actions, so one should not arrogantly impose obligations or restrictions on God.

Key Terms
الأصلح
تخليق الكفر
الحكمة
المصلحة
المفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that humans can rationally determine what actions are beneficial for God to do.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that human reason is limited; one cannot know the true benefit of divine actions, so we should not impose obligations on God.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazili objection to the idea that creating kufr could be in the servant's best interest?
A) It would make God unjust.
B) It is impossible because the servant would be harmed.
C) They say: How can that be most beneficial when the servant is harmed by it?
D) They have no objection.

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom is absolute; human reason cannot judge His actions as beneficial or harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a tragedy or hardship in your life, do you trust that God's wisdom is absolute, or do you secretly believe you could suggest a better plan? Which specific trial are you currently questioning God about? Commit today to recite 'I believe in the decree, its good and evil, and that my intellect is incapable of fully grasping His wisdom.'
?
The Mu'tazila tried to impose what they thought was best for others onto God. In your dealings with family, friends, or even in your own life, when do you find yourself acting as if you know what is truly beneficial for them, overriding God's decree? This week, in one concrete situation, choose to remain silent and trust God's plan instead of insisting on your own solution.
?
If God's creation of disbelief in someone could be wise even if we don't see it, then what right do you have to judge anyone's fate or to despair of God's mercy for yourself? Think of one person you have written off as hopeless or one sin you think God cannot forgive. In light of this lesson, surrender your judgment to God and ask Him to give you hope.

Refuting the Claim that God Must Create the Best
A dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must create the best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must create what is best for the servant, and why human reason cannot impose obligations on God.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not bound by human perceptions of benefit and harm.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that God is obliged to create the best for each servant. The author uses a series of logical reversals to show that the Mu'tazila position is inconsistent and that human intellect cannot fully grasp divine wisdom. As we read, we'll follow the back-and-forth dialectic and see how the author turns their own reasoning against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Let the author's own rhythm and structure come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that God must create what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for the servant. Notice how the author first challenges this by pointing out that even rational people perceive harm in some acts.
3
Trace the dialectic
Follow the back-and-forth: the author raises an example (creating disbelief), the Mu'tazila object, the author responds by showing inconsistency. Pay attention to the comparison with keeping the servant alive until disbelief.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm in God's actions. Consider why this is a powerful rebuttal against imposing obligations on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه المصلحة . فيقال لهم: بل يعقل فيه كل عاقل وجه المفسدة والمضرة، وعرف أن ما قلتم باطل.
ثم نقول لهم ما أنكرتم على من يقول لكم إذا ثبت بما بينا أنه تعالى يمنع أنه تعالى حكيم علم أن منع المصلحة والأصلح حكمة وإن كنا لا نعقل وجه الحكمة، ويقال لهم ما أنكرتم على هذا أن يكون تخليق الكفر أصلح للعبد؟ فإن قالوا: كيف يكون ذلك أصلح وقد يتضرر به العبد؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون الإبقاء إلى أن يكفر أو يرتد أصلح له وهو يتضرر به، وإن قالوا: لا يتصور من العبد الإيمان إذا خلق فيه الكفر . قلنا ولا يتصور منه الإيمان إذا علم أنه يكفر فإن كان هذا مصلحة فذاك أيضا مصلحة، وإن لم يكن ذاك مصلحة فهذا أيضا ليس بمصلحة وإن عقلا هذا مبلغ معرفته بالمصلحة والمفسدة لحقيق أن لا يعرف صاحبه به الحكمة والسفه ولحري أن لا يتحكم على الله بالإيجاب عليه تارة والحجر عليه أخرى، والله الموفق.
The aspect of benefit. So it is said to them: Rather every rational person perceives in it the aspect of harm and injury, and they know that what you said is false. Then we say to them: What do you deny from one who says to you: When it is established through what we have explained that He, exalted, prevents [the best]—that He, exalted, is wise, [and] He knows that preventing benefit and the best is wisdom even if we do not perceive the aspect of wisdom? And it is said to them: What do you deny from this—that creating disbelief is best for the servant? For if they say: How can that be best when the servant is harmed by it? We say: And how is keeping [him alive] until he disbelieves or apostatizes best for him while he is harmed by it? And if they say: Faith from the servant is inconceivable when disbelief is created in him. We say: Nor is faith conceivable from him when it is known that he will disbelieve. So if this is a benefit, then that also is a benefit; and if that is not a benefit, then this also is not a benefit. And if the intellect—this is the extent of its knowledge of benefit and harm—it is proper that its possessor should not know wisdom and foolishness, and it is fitting that he should not presume to impose obligations on God or restrict Him. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1003

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must always create what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for the servant. The author first points out that even the Mu'tazila admit that in some acts there is both benefit and harm, so their claim that God must avoid harm is inconsistent. He then turns their logic against them: if they say that creating disbelief cannot be best because it harms the servant, then why do they allow God to keep the servant alive until he disbelieves, which also harms him? They also claim that faith is impossible if disbelief is created in the servant, but the author retorts that faith is also impossible if God knows the servant will disbelieve. Thus, if one case is a benefit, the other is likewise; and if one is not, the other also is not. The author concludes that human intellect is too limited to judge what is truly beneficial or harmful for God, and that it is wrong to impose obligations on God or restrict Him.

Key Terms
مصلحة (benefit)
أصلح (best)
مفسدة (harm)
حكمة (wisdom)
تخليق الكفر (creating disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obligated to do what appears best to human reason.
Correction: The author shows that human reason cannot fully determine what is truly beneficial or harmful in God's actions, and that God's wisdom transcends our limited comprehension.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does the author highlight in the Mu'tazila position?
A) They accept that God keeps the servant alive until disbelief (which harms him) but reject creating disbelief as harmful.
B) They claim God is wise but deny His wisdom.
C) They say God must create the best, but also say He creates evil.
D) They argue from reason but reject revelation.

Key Takeaway
Human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm in God's actions, so we cannot impose that God must create what we deem best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your own perception of what is 'best' for you clashed with what actually happened, and how did you react? Do you still trust that God's wisdom surpasses your understanding, even when the outcome seems harmful?
?
The author argues that human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm. Think of a time you were certain something was harmful, only later to realize it was beneficial. If you truly believe God's wisdom is unlimited, what would change in how you respond to trials you don't understand?
?
This passage rebukes those who impose on God what He 'must' do. Search your heart: do you ever subconsciously expect God to act according to your sense of fairness or logic? What would it cost you to surrender that expectation entirely?
